<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Gustav Holst</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">English composer</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">



</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gustav_Holst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="middle aged man in head and shoulder shot looking warily at camera" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Gustav_Holst.jpg/240px-Gustav_Holst.jpg" width="240" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Gustav_Holst.jpg/360px-Gustav_Holst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Gustav_Holst.jpg/480px-Gustav_Holst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="485" data-file-height="629" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gustav_Holst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gustav Holst, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1921</span> (photograph by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Lambert" title="Herbert Lambert">Herbert Lambert</a>)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Gustav Theodore Holst</b> (born <b>Gustavus Theodore von Holst</b>; 21 September 1874&#160;– 25 May 1934) was an English composer, arranger and teacher. Best known for his orchestral suite <i><a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">The Planets</a></i>, he composed a large number of other works across a range of genres, although none achieved comparable success. His distinctive compositional style was the product of many influences, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a> being most crucial early in his development. The subsequent inspiration of the <a href="/wiki/English_folk_music#Folk_revivals_1890–1969" class="mw-redirect" title="English folk music">English folksong revival</a> of the early 20th century, and the example of such rising modern composers as <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Ravel" title="Maurice Ravel">Maurice Ravel</a>, led Holst to develop and refine an individual style.
</p><p>There were professional musicians in the previous three generations of Holst's family and it was clear from his early years that he would follow the same calling. He hoped to become a pianist, but was prevented by <a href="/wiki/Neuritis" title="Neuritis">neuritis</a> in his right arm. Despite his father's reservations, he pursued a career as a composer, studying at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Music" title="Royal College of Music">Royal College of Music</a> under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Villiers_Stanford" title="Charles Villiers Stanford">Charles Villiers Stanford</a>. Unable to support himself by his compositions, he played the <a href="/wiki/Trombone" title="Trombone">trombone</a> professionally and later became a teacher—a great one, according to his colleague <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a>. Among other teaching activities he built up a strong tradition of performance at <a href="/wiki/Morley_College" title="Morley College">Morley College</a>, where he served as musical director from 1907 until 1924, and pioneered music education for women at <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School" title="St Paul&#39;s Girls&#39; School">St Paul's Girls' School</a>, where he taught from 1905 until his death in 1934. He was the founder of a series of <a href="/wiki/Whitsun" title="Whitsun">Whitsun</a> music festivals, which ran from 1916 for the remainder of his life.
</p><p>Holst's works were played frequently in the early years of the 20th century, but it was not until the international success of <i>The Planets</i> in the years immediately after the First World War that he became a well-known figure. A shy man, he did not welcome this fame, and preferred to be left in peace to compose and teach. In his later years his uncompromising, personal style of composition struck many music lovers as too austere, and his brief popularity declined. Nevertheless, he was a significant influence on a number of younger English composers, including <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Rubbra" title="Edmund Rubbra">Edmund Rubbra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tippett" title="Michael Tippett">Michael Tippett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Britten" title="Benjamin Britten">Benjamin Britten</a>. Apart from <i>The Planets</i> and a handful of other works, his music was generally neglected until the 1980s, when recordings of much of his output became available.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background">Family background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Family background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-family-tree.tif" class="image"><img alt="family tree diagram showing Gustav in relation to three earlier generations" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Holst-family-tree.tif/lossless-page1-310px-Holst-family-tree.tif.png" width="310" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Holst-family-tree.tif/lossless-page1-465px-Holst-family-tree.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Holst-family-tree.tif/lossless-page1-620px-Holst-family-tree.tif.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="422" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-family-tree.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Holst family tree (simplified)</center></div></div></div>
<p>Holst was born in <a href="/wiki/Cheltenham" title="Cheltenham">Cheltenham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, the elder of the two children of Adolph von Holst, a professional musician, and his wife, Clara Cox, <i>née</i> Lediard. She was of mostly British descent,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup> daughter of a respected <a href="/wiki/Cirencester" title="Cirencester">Cirencester</a> solicitor;<sup id="cite_ref-m3_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m3-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> the Holst side of the family was of mixed Swedish, Latvian and German ancestry, with at least one professional musician in each of the previous three generations.<sup id="cite_ref-m2_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m2-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst's great-grandfather, Matthias Holst, born in Riga, Latvia, <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Germans" title="Baltic Germans">was of German origin</a>; he served as composer and harp-teacher to the Imperial Russian Court in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">St Petersburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Matthias's son Gustavus, who moved to England with his parents as a child in 1802,<sup id="cite_ref-short9_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short9-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> was a composer of salon-style music and a well-known harp teacher. He appropriated the aristocratic prefix "von" and added it to the family name in the hope of gaining enhanced prestige and attracting pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst's father, Adolph von Holst, became organist and choirmaster at <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Church,_Cheltenham" title="All Saints&#39; Church, Cheltenham">All Saints' Church</a>, Cheltenham;<sup id="cite_ref-Short,_p._10_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Short,_p._10-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> he also taught, and gave piano recitals.<sup id="cite_ref-Short,_p._10_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Short,_p._10-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His wife, Clara, a former pupil, was a talented singer and pianist. They had two sons; Gustav's younger brother, Emil Gottfried, became known as <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Cossart" title="Ernest Cossart">Ernest Cossart</a>, a successful actor in the <a href="/wiki/West_End_theatre" title="West End theatre">West End</a>, New York and <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Clara died in February 1882, and the family moved to another house in Cheltenham,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup> where Adolph recruited his sister Nina to help raise the boys. Gustav recognised her devotion to the family and dedicated several of his early compositions to her.<sup id="cite_ref-m3_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m3-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In 1885 Adolph married Mary Thorley Stone, another of his pupils. They had two sons, Matthias (known as "Max") and Evelyn ("Thorley").<sup id="cite_ref-m34_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m34-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Mary von Holst was absorbed in <a href="/wiki/Theosophy_(Blavatskian)" title="Theosophy (Blavatskian)">theosophy</a> and not greatly interested in domestic matters. All four of Adolph's sons were subject to what one biographer calls "benign neglect",<sup id="cite_ref-m34_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m34-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and Gustav in particular was "not overburdened with attention or understanding, with a weak sight and a weak chest, both neglected—he was 'miserable and scared'."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_youth">Childhood and youth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Childhood and youth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holst was taught to play the piano and the violin; he enjoyed the former but hated the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-h19697_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h19697-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> At the age of twelve he took up the trombone at Adolph's suggestion, thinking that playing a brass instrument might improve his <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-timesobit_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesobit-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Holst was educated at <a href="/wiki/Pate%27s_Grammar_School" title="Pate&#39;s Grammar School">Cheltenham Grammar School</a> between 1886 and 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He started composing in or about 1886; inspired by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Babington_Macaulay,_1st_Baron_Macaulay" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Babington Macaulay, 1st Baron Macaulay">Macaulay</a>'s poem <i><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Horatius" class="extiw" title="s:Horatius">Horatius</a></i> he began, but soon abandoned, an ambitious setting of the work for chorus and orchestra.<sup id="cite_ref-h19697_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h19697-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> His early compositions included piano pieces, organ voluntaries, songs, anthems and a symphony (from 1892). His main influences at this stage were <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Mendelssohn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin" title="Frédéric Chopin">Chopin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Grieg" title="Edvard Grieg">Grieg</a> and above all <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan" title="Arthur Sullivan">Sullivan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Adolph tried to steer his son away from composition, hoping that he would have a career as a pianist. Holst was oversensitive and miserable. His eyes were weak, but no one realized that he needed to wear spectacles. Holst's health played a decisive part in his musical future; he had never been strong, and in addition to his asthma and poor eyesight he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Neuritis" title="Neuritis">neuritis</a>, which made playing the piano difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He said that the affected arm was "like a jelly overcharged with electricity".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Holst left school in 1891, Adolph paid for him to spend four months in Oxford studying <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a> with George Frederick Sims, organist of <a href="/wiki/Merton_College,_Oxford" title="Merton College, Oxford">Merton College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> On his return, Holst obtained his first professional appointment, aged seventeen, as organist and choirmaster at <a href="/wiki/Wyck_Rissington" title="Wyck Rissington">Wyck Rissington</a>, Gloucestershire. The post brought with it the conductorship of the <a href="/wiki/Bourton-on-the-Water" title="Bourton-on-the-Water">Bourton-on-the-Water</a> Choral Society, which offered no extra remuneration but provided valuable experience that enabled him to hone his conducting skills.<sup id="cite_ref-h19697_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h19697-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In November 1891 Holst gave what was perhaps his first public performance as a pianist; he and his father played the <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Dances_(Brahms)" title="Hungarian Dances (Brahms)">Hungarian Dances</a></i> at a concert in Cheltenham.<sup id="cite_ref-m6_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m6-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The programme for the event gives his name as "Gustav" rather than "Gustavus"; he was called by the shorter version from his early years.<sup id="cite_ref-m6_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m6-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_College_of_Music">Royal College of Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Royal College of Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1892 Holst wrote the music for an operetta in the style of <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a>, <i>Lansdown Castle, or The Sorcerer of Tewkesbury</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The piece was performed at Cheltenham Corn Exchange in February 1893; it was well received and its success encouraged him to persevere with composing.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> He applied for a scholarship at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Music" title="Royal College of Music">Royal College of Music</a> (RCM) in London, but the composition scholarship for that year was won by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Coleridge-Taylor" title="Samuel Coleridge-Taylor">Samuel Coleridge-Taylor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h19698_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h19698-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Holst was accepted as a non-scholarship student, and Adolph borrowed £100 to cover the first year's expenses.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup> Holst left Cheltenham for London in May 1893. Money was tight, and partly from frugality and partly from his own inclination he became a vegetarian and a teetotaller.<sup id="cite_ref-h19698_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h19698-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Two years later he was finally granted a scholarship, which slightly eased his financial difficulties, but he retained his austere personal regime.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:119px;max-width:119px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:117px;max-width:117px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg/115px-Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg" width="115" height="138" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg/173px-Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg/230px-Stanford-Bassano-1921-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="315" data-file-height="379" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Charles_Villiers_Stanford" title="Charles Villiers Stanford">Charles Villiers Stanford</a>, Holst's composition professor</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:117px;max-width:117px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg/115px-Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg" width="115" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg/173px-Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg/230px-Vaughan_Williams_by_Rothenstein.jpg 2x" data-file-width="315" data-file-height="370" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Holst's lifelong friend <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Holst's professors at the RCM were Frederick Sharpe (piano), William Stephenson Hoyte (organ), George Case (trombone),<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;n 6&#93;</a></sup> George Jacobi (instrumentation) and the director of the college, <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Parry" title="Hubert Parry">Hubert Parry</a> (history). After preliminary lessons with <a href="/wiki/W._S._Rockstro" title="W. S. Rockstro">W. S. Rockstro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Bridge" title="Frederick Bridge">Frederick Bridge</a>, Holst was granted his wish to study composition with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Villiers_Stanford" title="Charles Villiers Stanford">Charles Villiers Stanford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>To support himself during his studies Holst played the trombone professionally, at seaside resorts in the summer and in London theatres in the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-h198119_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198119-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> His daughter and biographer, <a href="/wiki/Imogen_Holst" title="Imogen Holst">Imogen Holst</a>, records that from his fees as a player "he was able to afford the necessities of life: board and lodging, manuscript paper, and tickets for standing room in the gallery at Covent Garden Opera House on Wagner evenings".<sup id="cite_ref-h198119_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198119-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> He secured an occasional engagement in symphony concerts, playing in 1897 under the baton of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Hall" title="Queen&#39;s Hall">Queen's Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like many musicians of his generation, Holst came under <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Wagner</a>'s spell. He had recoiled from the music of <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6tterd%C3%A4mmerung" title="Götterdämmerung">Götterdämmerung</a></i> when he heard it at Covent Garden in 1892, but encouraged by his friend and fellow-student <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Hart" title="Fritz Hart">Fritz Hart</a> he persevered and quickly became an ardent Wagnerite.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Wagner supplanted Sullivan as the main influence on his music,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and for some time, as Imogen put it, "ill-assimilated wisps of <i><a href="/wiki/Tristan_und_Isolde" title="Tristan und Isolde">Tristan</a></i> inserted themselves on nearly every page of his own songs and overtures."<sup id="cite_ref-h198119_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198119-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Stanford admired some of Wagner's works, and had in his earlier years been influenced by him,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> but Holst's sub-Wagnerian compositions met with his disapprobation: "It won't do, me boy; it won't do".<sup id="cite_ref-h198119_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198119-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Holst respected Stanford, describing him to a fellow-pupil, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Howells" title="Herbert Howells">Herbert Howells</a>, as "the one man who could get any one of us out of a technical mess",<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> but he found that his fellow students, rather than the faculty members, had the greater influence on his development.<sup id="cite_ref-h198119_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198119-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1895, shortly after celebrating his twenty-first birthday, Holst met <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a>, who became a lifelong friend and had more influence on Holst's music than anybody else.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Stanford emphasised the need for his students to be self-critical, but Holst and Vaughan Williams became one another's chief critics; each would play his latest composition to the other while still working on it. Vaughan Williams later observed, "What one really learns from an Academy or College is not so much from one's official teachers as from one's fellow-students&#160;... [we discussed] every subject under the sun from the lowest note of the double bassoon to the philosophy of <i><a href="/wiki/Jude_the_Obscure" title="Jude the Obscure">Jude the Obscure</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In 1949 he wrote of their relationship, "Holst declared that his music was influenced by that of his friend: the converse is certainly true."<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The year 1895 was also the bicentenary of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Henry Purcell</a>, which was marked by various performances including Stanford conducting <i><a href="/wiki/Dido_and_Aeneas" title="Dido and Aeneas">Dido and Aeneas</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_Theatre,_London" title="Lyceum Theatre, London">Lyceum Theatre</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> the work profoundly impressed Holst,<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> who over twenty years later confessed to a friend that his search for "the (or <u>a</u>) musical idiom of the English language" had been inspired "unconsciously" by "hearing the <a href="/wiki/Recitative" title="Recitative">recits</a> in Purcell's <i>Dido</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-GHolstWhit23_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GHolstWhit23-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another early influence was <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h196916_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h196916-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In Vaughan Williams's words, "It was now that Holst discovered the feeling of unity with his fellow men which made him afterwards a great teacher. A sense of comradeship rather than political conviction led him, while still a student, to join the Kelmscott House <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">Socialist</a> Club in Hammersmith."<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Kelmscott_House" title="Kelmscott House">Kelmscott House</a>, Morris's home, Holst attended lectures by his host and <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">Bernard Shaw</a>. His own socialism was moderate in character, but he enjoyed the club for its good company and his admiration of Morris as a man.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> His ideals were influenced by Morris's but had a different emphasis. Morris had written, "I do not want art for a few any more than education for a few, or freedom for a few. I want all persons to be educated according to their capacity, not according to the amount of money which their parents happen to have".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Holst said, "'Aristocracy in art'—art is not for all but only for the chosen few—but the only way to find those few is to bring art to everyone—then the artists have a sort of masonic signal by which they recognise each other in the crowd."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;n 7&#93;</a></sup> He was invited to conduct the Hammersmith Socialist Choir, teaching them <a href="/wiki/Madrigals" class="mw-redirect" title="Madrigals">madrigals</a> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Morley" title="Thomas Morley">Thomas Morley</a>, choruses by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Purcell</a>, and works by <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a>, Wagner and himself.<sup id="cite_ref-ih198123_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ih198123-49">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> One of his choristers was (Emily) Isobel Harrison (1876–1969), a beautiful <a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">soprano</a> two years his junior. He fell in love with her; she was at first unimpressed by him, but she came round and they were engaged, though with no immediate prospect of marriage given Holst's tiny income.<sup id="cite_ref-ih198123_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ih198123-49">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_musician">Professional musician</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Professional musician">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HolstStatue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="outdoor full length statue showing Holst conducting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/HolstStatue.jpg/170px-HolstStatue.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/HolstStatue.jpg/255px-HolstStatue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/HolstStatue.jpg/340px-HolstStatue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1532" data-file-height="1884" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HolstStatue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Holst at his birthplace, <a href="/wiki/Cheltenham" title="Cheltenham">Cheltenham</a>. He is shown with the baton in his left hand, his frequent practice because of the <a href="/wiki/Neuritis" title="Neuritis">neuritis</a> in his right arm.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1898 the RCM offered Holst a further year's scholarship, but he felt that he had learned as much as he could there and that it was time, as he put it, to "learn by doing".<sup id="cite_ref-ih198123_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ih198123-49">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Some of his compositions were published and performed; the previous year <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> had praised his song "Light Leaves Whisper", "a moderately elaborate composition in six parts, treated with a good deal of expression and poetic feeling".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Occasional successes notwithstanding, Holst found that "man cannot live by composition alone";<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> he took posts as organist at various London churches, and continued playing the trombone in theatre orchestras. In 1898 he was appointed first trombonist and <i><a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9p%C3%A9titeur" title="Répétiteur">répétiteur</a></i> with the <a href="/wiki/Carl_Rosa_Opera_Company" title="Carl Rosa Opera Company">Carl Rosa Opera Company</a> and toured with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scottish_National_Orchestra" title="Royal Scottish National Orchestra">Scottish Orchestra</a>. Though a capable rather than a virtuoso player he won the praise of the leading conductor <a href="/wiki/Hans_Richter_(conductor)" title="Hans Richter (conductor)">Hans Richter</a>, for whom he played at Covent Garden.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> His salary was only just enough to live on,<sup id="cite_ref-ih198127_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ih198127-53">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and he supplemented it by playing in a popular orchestra called the "White Viennese Band", conducted by Stanislas Wurm.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Holst enjoyed playing for Wurm, and learned much from him about drawing <a href="/wiki/Rubato" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubato">rubato</a> from players.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;n 8&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, longing to devote his time to composing, Holst found the necessity of playing for "the Worm" or any other light orchestra "a wicked and loathsome waste of time".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Vaughan Williams did not altogether agree with his friend about this; he admitted that some of the music was "trashy" but thought it had been useful to Holst nonetheless: "To start with, the very worst a trombonist has to put up with is as nothing compared to what a church organist has to endure; and secondly, Holst is above all an orchestral composer, and that sure touch which distinguishes his orchestral writing is due largely to the fact that he has been an orchestral player; he has learnt his art, both technically and in substance, not at second hand from text books and models, but from actual live experience."<sup id="cite_ref-vwml_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vwml-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With a modest income secured, Holst was able to marry Isobel; the ceremony was at Fulham Register Office on 22 June 1901. Their marriage lasted until his death; there was one child, <a href="/wiki/Imogen_Holst" title="Imogen Holst">Imogen</a>, born in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In 1902 <a href="/wiki/Dan_Godfrey" title="Dan Godfrey">Dan Godfrey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bournemouth_Symphony_Orchestra" title="Bournemouth Symphony Orchestra">Bournemouth Municipal Orchestra</a> premiered Holst's symphony <i>The Cotswolds</i> (Op. 8), the slow movement of which is a lament for William Morris who had died in October 1896, three years before Holst began work on the piece.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1903 Adolph von Holst died, leaving a small legacy. Holst and his wife decided, as Imogen later put it, that "as they were always hard up the only thing to do was to spend it all at once on a holiday in Germany".<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composer_and_teacher">Composer and teacher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Composer and teacher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School,_London_03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="commemorative plaque to Holst" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School%2C_London_03.jpg/170px-St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School%2C_London_03.jpg" width="170" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School%2C_London_03.jpg/255px-St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School%2C_London_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School%2C_London_03.jpg/340px-St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School%2C_London_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2439" data-file-height="2371" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School,_London_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue plaque at <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School" title="St Paul&#39;s Girls&#39; School">St Paul's Girls' School</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>While in Germany, Holst reappraised his professional life, and in 1903 he decided to abandon orchestral playing to concentrate on composition.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His earnings as a composer were too little to live on, and two years later he accepted the offer of a teaching post at <a href="/wiki/James_Allen%27s_Girls%27_School" title="James Allen&#39;s Girls&#39; School">James Allen's Girls' School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dulwich" title="Dulwich">Dulwich</a>, which he held until 1921. He also taught at the <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ward_Centre" title="Mary Ward Centre">Passmore Edwards Settlement</a>, where among other innovations he gave the British premieres of two Bach cantatas.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The two teaching posts for which he is probably best known were director of music at <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Girls%27_School" title="St Paul&#39;s Girls&#39; School">St Paul's Girls' School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith" title="Hammersmith">Hammersmith</a>, from 1905 until his death, and director of music at <a href="/wiki/Morley_College" title="Morley College">Morley College</a> from 1907 to 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Vaughan Williams wrote of the former establishment: "Here he did away with the childish sentimentality which schoolgirls were supposed to appreciate and substituted Bach and <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1s_Luis_de_Victoria" title="Tomás Luis de Victoria">Vittoria</a>; a splendid background for immature minds."<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Several of Holst's pupils at St Paul's went on to distinguished careers, including the soprano <a href="/wiki/Joan_Cross" title="Joan Cross">Joan Cross</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and the oboist and <a href="/wiki/Cor_anglais" title="Cor anglais">cor anglais</a> player Helen Gaskell.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Of Holst's impact on Morley College, Vaughan Williams wrote: "[A] bad tradition had to be broken down. The results were at first discouraging, but soon a new spirit appeared and the music of Morley College, together with its offshoot the 'Whitsuntide festival'&#160;... became a force to be reckoned with".<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Before Holst's appointment, Morley College had not treated music very seriously (Vaughan Williams's "bad tradition"), and at first Holst's exacting demands drove many students away. He persevered, and gradually built up a class of dedicated music-lovers.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the composer <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Rubbra" title="Edmund Rubbra">Edmund Rubbra</a>, who studied under him in the early 1920s, Holst was "a teacher who often came to lessons weighted, not with the learning of <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Prout" title="Ebenezer Prout">Prout</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Stainer" title="John Stainer">Stainer</a>, but with a miniature score of <i><a href="/wiki/Petrushka" title="Petrushka">Petrushka</a></i> or the then recently published <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_G_minor_(Vaughan_Williams)" title="Mass in G minor (Vaughan Williams)">Mass in G minor</a> of Vaughan Williams".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> He never sought to impose his own ideas on his composition pupils. Rubbra recalled that he would divine a student's difficulties and gently guide him to finding the solution for himself. "I do not recall that Holst added one single note of his own to anything I wrote, but he would suggest—if I agreed!—that, given such and such a phrase, the following one would be better if it took such and such a course; if I did not see this, the point would not be insisted upon&#160;... He frequently took away [because of] his abhorrence of unessentials."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif" class="image"><img alt="mug shots of four literary luminaries from the 19th and 20th centuries" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3c/Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3c/Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3c/Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muller-Whitman-Hardy-Bridges.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Literary influences, from top left clockwise: <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman" title="Walt Whitman">Walt Whitman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bridges" title="Robert Bridges">Robert Bridges</a></div></div></div>
<p>As a composer Holst was frequently inspired by literature. He set poetry by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bridges" title="Robert Bridges">Robert Bridges</a> and, a particular influence, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman" title="Walt Whitman">Walt Whitman</a>, whose words he set in "Dirge for Two Veterans" and <i>The Mystic Trumpeter</i> (1904). He wrote an orchestral <i>Walt Whitman Overture</i> in 1899.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> While on tour with the Carl Rosa company Holst had read some of <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a>'s books, which inspired in him a keen interest in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> texts, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rig Veda</a> hymns.<sup id="cite_ref-r30_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r30-67">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He found the existing English versions of the texts unconvincing,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;n 9&#93;</a></sup> and decided to make his own translations, despite his lack of skills as a linguist. He enrolled in 1909 at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College, London</a>, to study the language.<sup id="cite_ref-h24_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h24-68">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Imogen commented on his translations: "He was not a poet, and there are occasions when his verses seem naïve. But they never sound vague or slovenly, for he had set himself the task of finding words that would be 'clear and dignified' and that would 'lead the listener into another world'".<sup id="cite_ref-h198125_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198125-70">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> His settings of translations of Sanskrit texts included <i>Sita</i> (1899–1906), a three-act opera based on an episode in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i> (which he eventually entered for a competition for English opera set by the Milan music publisher <a href="/wiki/Casa_Ricordi" title="Casa Ricordi">Tito Ricordi</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Savitri_(opera)" title="Savitri (opera)">Savitri</a></i> (1908), a <a href="/wiki/Chamber_opera" title="Chamber opera">chamber opera</a> based on a tale from the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>; four groups of <i>Hymns from the Rig Veda</i> (1908–14); and two texts originally by <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81lid%C4%81sa" title="Kālidāsa">Kālidāsa</a>: <i>Two Eastern Pictures</i> (1909–10) and <i>The Cloud Messenger</i> (1913).<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Towards the end of the nineteenth century, British musical circles had experienced a new interest in national folk music. Some composers, such as Sullivan and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Elgar</a>, remained indifferent,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> but Parry, Stanford, Stainer and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mackenzie_(composer)" title="Alexander Mackenzie (composer)">Alexander Mackenzie</a> were founding members of the <a href="/wiki/English_Folk_Dance_and_Song_Society" title="English Folk Dance and Song Society">Folk-Song Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-graebe_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graebe-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Parry considered that by recovering English folk song, English composers would find an authentic national voice; he commented, "in true folk-songs there is no sham, no got-up glitter, and no vulgarity".<sup id="cite_ref-graebe_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graebe-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Vaughan Williams was an early and enthusiastic convert to this cause, going round the English countryside collecting and noting down folk songs. These had an influence on Holst. Though not as passionate on the subject as his friend, he incorporated a number of folk melodies in his own compositions and made several arrangements of folk songs collected by others.<sup id="cite_ref-graebe_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graebe-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Somerset Rhapsody</i> (1906–07), was written at the suggestion of the folk-song collector <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Sharp" title="Cecil Sharp">Cecil Sharp</a> and made use of tunes that Sharp had noted down. Holst described its performance at the Queen's Hall in 1910 as "my first real success".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A few years later Holst became excited by another musical renaissance—the rediscovery of English madrigal composers. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Weelkes" title="Thomas Weelkes">Weelkes</a> was his favourite of all the Tudor composers, but <a href="/wiki/William_Byrd" title="William Byrd">Byrd</a> also meant much to him.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_on_The_Terrace,_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg" class="image"><img alt="exterior of small, pretty early 19th-century house" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/House_on_The_Terrace%2C_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg/170px-House_on_The_Terrace%2C_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/House_on_The_Terrace%2C_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg/255px-House_on_The_Terrace%2C_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/House_on_The_Terrace%2C_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg/340px-House_on_The_Terrace%2C_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg 2x" data-file-width="427" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_on_The_Terrace,_Barnes_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1309706.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The house in <a href="/wiki/Barnes,_London" title="Barnes, London">Barnes</a> where Holst lived between 1908 and 1913. A commemorative <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> is fixed to the front</div></div></div>
<p>Holst was a keen <a href="/wiki/Ramblers" class="mw-redirect" title="Ramblers">rambler</a>. He walked extensively in England, Italy, France and Algeria. In 1908 he travelled to Algeria on medical advice as a treatment for asthma and the depression that he suffered after his opera <i>Sita</i> failed to win the Ricordi prize.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> This trip inspired the suite <i><a href="/wiki/Beni_Mora" title="Beni Mora">Beni Mora</a></i>, which incorporated music he heard in the Algerian streets.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Vaughan Williams wrote of this exotic work, "if it had been played in Paris rather than London it would have given its composer a European reputation, and played in Italy would probably have caused a riot."<sup id="cite_ref-ml2_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ml2-78">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1910s">1910s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1910s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 1911 Holst and his Morley College students gave the first performance since the seventeenth century of Purcell's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fairy-Queen" title="The Fairy-Queen">The Fairy-Queen</a></i>. The full score had been lost soon after Purcell's death in 1695, and had only recently been found. Twenty-eight Morley students copied out the complete vocal and orchestral parts. There were 1,500 pages of music and it took the students almost eighteen months to copy them out in their spare time.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> A concert performance of the work was given at <a href="/wiki/The_Old_Vic" title="The Old Vic">The Old Vic</a>, preceded by an introductory talk by Vaughan Williams. <i>The Times</i> praised Holst and his forces for "a most interesting and artistic performance of this very important work".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After this success, Holst was disappointed the following year by the lukewarm reception of his choral work <i>The Cloud Messenger</i>. He again went travelling, accepting an invitation from <a href="/wiki/H._Balfour_Gardiner" title="H. Balfour Gardiner">H. Balfour Gardiner</a> to join him and the brothers <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Bax" title="Clifford Bax">Clifford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Bax" title="Arnold Bax">Arnold Bax</a> in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> During this holiday Clifford Bax introduced Holst to <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>, an interest that later inspired his suite <i><a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">The Planets</a></i>. Holst cast his friends' <a href="/wiki/Horoscope" title="Horoscope">horoscopes</a> for the rest of his life and referred to astrology as his "pet vice".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1913, St Paul's Girls' School opened a new music wing, and Holst composed <i><a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Suite" title="St Paul&#39;s Suite">St Paul's Suite</a></i> for the occasion. The new building contained a sound-proof room, handsomely equipped, where he could work undisturbed.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Holst and his family moved to a house in <a href="/wiki/Brook_Green" title="Brook Green">Brook Green</a>, very close to the school. For the previous six years they had lived in a pretty house overlooking the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">Thames</a> at <a href="/wiki/Barnes,_London" title="Barnes, London">Barnes</a>, but the river air, frequently foggy, affected his breathing.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> For use at weekends and during school holidays, Holst and his wife bought a cottage in <a href="/wiki/Thaxted" title="Thaxted">Thaxted</a>, Essex, surrounded by mediaeval buildings and ample rambling opportunities.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In 1917 they moved to a house in the centre of the town, where they stayed until 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg" class="image"><img alt="exterior of house in country town" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg/220px-The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg/330px-The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg/440px-The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manse_-_geograph.org.uk_-_845330.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Manse in Thaxted where Holst lived from 1917 to 1925</div></div></div>
<p>At Thaxted, Holst became friendly with the Rev <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Noel" title="Conrad Noel">Conrad Noel</a>, known as the "Red Vicar", who supported the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> and espoused many causes unpopular with conservative opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Noel also encouraged the revival of folk-dancing and processionals as part of church ceremonies, innovations which caused controversy among traditionally-minded churchgoers.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Holst became an occasional organist and choirmaster at Thaxted Parish Church; he also developed an interest in bell-ringing.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;n 10&#93;</a></sup> He started an annual music festival at Whitsuntide in 1916; students from Morley College and St Paul's Girls' School performed together with local participants.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Holst's <i><a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a></i> carol, "This Have I Done For My True Love", was dedicated to Noel in recognition of his interest in the ancient origins of religion (the composer always referred to the work as "The Dancing Day").<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> It received its first performance during the Third Whitsun Festival at Thaxted in May 1918. During that festival, Noel, a staunch supporter of Russia's <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a>, demanded in a Saturday message during the service that there should be a greater political commitment from those who participated in the church activities; his claim that several of Holst's pupils (implicitly those from St Paul's Girls' School) were merely "camp followers" caused offence.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Holst, anxious to protect his students from being embroiled in ecclesiastical conflict, moved the Whitsun Festival to Dulwich, though he himself continued to help with the Thaxted choir and to play the church organ on occasion.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War">First World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: First World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the outbreak of the First World War, Holst tried to enlist but was rejected as unfit for military service.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He felt frustrated that he could not contribute to the war effort. His wife became a volunteer ambulance driver; Vaughan Williams went on active service to France as did Holst's brother Emil; Holst's friends the composers <a href="/wiki/George_Butterworth" title="George Butterworth">George Butterworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Coles" title="Cecil Coles">Cecil Coles</a> were killed in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> He continued to teach and compose; he worked on <i>The Planets</i> and prepared his chamber opera <i><a href="/wiki/Savitri_(opera)" title="Savitri (opera)">Savitri</a></i> for performance. It was first given in December 1916 by students of the London School of Opera at the Wellington Hall in <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_Wood" title="St John&#39;s Wood">St John's Wood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> It attracted no attention at the time from the main newspapers, though when professionally staged five years later it was greeted as "a perfect little masterpiece."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 1917 he wrote <i>The Hymn of Jesus</i> for chorus and orchestra, a work which remained unperformed until after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1918, as the war neared its end, Holst finally had the prospect of a job that offered him the chance to serve. The music section of the <a href="/wiki/YMCA" title="YMCA">YMCA</a>'s education department needed volunteers to work with British troops stationed in Europe awaiting demobilisation.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Morley College and St Paul's Girls' School offered him a year's leave of absence, but there remained one obstacle: the YMCA felt that his surname looked too German to be acceptable in such a role.<sup id="cite_ref-h196952_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h196952-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> He formally changed "von Holst" to "Holst" by <a href="/wiki/Deed_poll" title="Deed poll">deed poll</a> in September 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> He was appointed as the YMCA's musical organiser for the Near East, based in <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Salonica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-planets-inscription.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Handwritten inscription: &quot;This copy is the property of Adrian Boult, who first caused the Planets to shine in public and thereby earned the gratitude of Gustav Holst&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Holst-planets-inscription.jpg/310px-Holst-planets-inscription.jpg" width="310" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Holst-planets-inscription.jpg/465px-Holst-planets-inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Holst-planets-inscription.jpg/620px-Holst-planets-inscription.jpg 2x" data-file-width="978" data-file-height="563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-planets-inscription.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Holst's inscription on <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Boult" title="Adrian Boult">Adrian Boult</a>'s score of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">The Planets</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Holst was given a spectacular send-off. The conductor <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Boult" title="Adrian Boult">Adrian Boult</a> recalled, "Just before the Armistice, Gustav Holst burst into my office: 'Adrian, the YMCA are sending me to Salonica quite soon and Balfour Gardiner, bless his heart, has given me a parting present consisting of the Queen's Hall, full of the Queen's Hall Orchestra for the whole of a Sunday morning. So we're going to do <i>The Planets</i>, and you've got to conduct'."<sup id="cite_ref-b35_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b35-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> There was a burst of activity to get things ready in time. The girls at St Paul's helped to copy out the orchestral parts,<sup id="cite_ref-b35_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b35-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and the women of Morley and the St Paul's girls learned the choral part in the last movement.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The performance was given on 29 September to an invited audience including <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wood" title="Henry Wood">Sir Henry Wood</a> and most of the professional musicians in London.<sup id="cite_ref-m165_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m165-103">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Five months later, when Holst was in Greece, Boult introduced <i>The Planets</i> to the general public, at a concert in February 1919; Holst sent him a long letter full of suggestions,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;n 11&#93;</a></sup> but failed to convince him that the suite should be played in full. The conductor believed that about half an hour of such radically new music was all the public could absorb at first hearing, and he gave only five of the seven movements on that occasion.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst enjoyed his time in Salonica, from where he was able to visit Athens, which greatly impressed him.<sup id="cite_ref-s171_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s171-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> His musical duties were wide-ranging, and even obliged him on occasion to play the violin in the local orchestra: "it was great fun, but I fear I was not of much use".<sup id="cite_ref-s171_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s171-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> He returned to England in June 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On his return from Greece, Holst resumed his teaching and composing. In addition to his existing work he accepted a lectureship in composition at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Reading" title="University of Reading">University of Reading</a> and joined Vaughan Williams in teaching composition at their <i>alma mater</i> the RCM.<sup id="cite_ref-graebe_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graebe-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Inspired by Adrian Boult's conducting classes at the RCM, Holst tried to further pioneer music education for women by proposing to the High Mistress of St Paul's Girls' School that he should invite Boult to give classes at the school: "It would be glorious if the SPGS turned out the only women conductors in the world!"<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In his soundproof room at SPGS he composed the <i><a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Death" title="Ode to Death">Ode to Death</a></i>, a setting of a poem by Whitman, which according to Vaughan Williams is considered by many to be Holst's most beautiful choral work.<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/Holst-1921.jpg/170px-Holst-1921.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/Holst-1921.jpg/255px-Holst-1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/Holst-1921.jpg/340px-Holst-1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="506" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Holst, caricatured as "The Bringer of Jollity", by F Sanchez, 1921</div></div></div>
<p>Holst, in his forties, suddenly found himself in demand. The <a href="/wiki/New_York_Philharmonic" title="New York Philharmonic">New York Philharmonic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Symphony_Orchestra" title="Chicago Symphony Orchestra">Chicago Symphony Orchestra</a> vied to be the first to play <i>The Planets</i> in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-graebe_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graebe-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The success of that work was followed in 1920 by an enthusiastic reception for <i>The Hymn of Jesus</i>, described in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> as "one of the most brilliant and one of the most sincere pieces of choral and orchestral expression heard for some years."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Times</i> called it "undoubtedly the most strikingly original choral work which has been produced in this country for many years."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>To his surprise and dismay Holst was becoming famous.<sup id="cite_ref-archive_41-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Celebrity was something wholly foreign to his nature. As the music scholar <a href="/wiki/Byron_Adams" title="Byron Adams">Byron Adams</a> puts it, "he struggled for the rest of his life to extricate himself from the web of garish publicity, public incomprehension and professional envy woven about him by this unsought-for success."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> He turned down honours and awards proffered to him,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;n 12&#93;</a></sup> and refused to grant interviews or sign autographs.<sup id="cite_ref-graebe_73-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graebe-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst's comic opera <i><a href="/wiki/The_Perfect_Fool" title="The Perfect Fool">The Perfect Fool</a></i> (1923) was widely seen as a satire of <i><a href="/wiki/Parsifal" title="Parsifal">Parsifal</a></i>, though Holst firmly denied it.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The piece, with <a href="/wiki/Maggie_Teyte" title="Maggie Teyte">Maggie Teyte</a> in the leading soprano role and <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Aynsley_Goossens" title="Eugene Aynsley Goossens">Eugene Goossens</a> conducting, was enthusiastically received at its premiere in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House" title="Royal Opera House">Royal Opera House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-timespf_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timespf-115">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> At a concert in Reading in 1923, Holst slipped and fell, suffering <a href="/wiki/Concussion" title="Concussion">concussion</a>. He seemed to make a good recovery, and he felt up to accepting an invitation to the US, lecturing and conducting at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> After he returned he found himself more and more in demand, to conduct, prepare his earlier works for publication, and, as before, to teach. The strain caused by these demands on him was too great; on doctor's orders he cancelled all professional engagements during 1924, and retreated to Thaxted.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In 1925 he resumed his work at St Paul's Girls' School, but did not return to any of his other posts.<sup id="cite_ref-h198164_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198164-118">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Holst's productivity as a composer benefited almost at once from his release from other work. His works from this period include the <i><a href="/wiki/Choral_Symphony_(Holst)" title="Choral Symphony (Holst)">Choral Symphony</a></i> to words by <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">Keats</a> (a <i>Second Choral Symphony</i> to words by George Meredith exists only in fragments). A short Shakespearian opera, <i><a href="/wiki/At_the_Boar%27s_Head" title="At the Boar&#39;s Head">At the Boar's Head</a></i>, followed; neither had the immediate popular appeal of <i>A Moorside Suite</i> for brass band of 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1927 Holst was commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Symphony_Orchestra" title="New York Symphony Orchestra">New York Symphony Orchestra</a> to write a symphony. Instead, he wrote an orchestral piece <i><a href="/wiki/Egdon_Heath_(Holst)" title="Egdon Heath (Holst)">Egdon Heath</a></i>, inspired by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy%27s_Wessex" title="Thomas Hardy&#39;s Wessex">Thomas Hardy's Wessex</a>. It was first performed in February 1928, a month after Hardy's death, at a memorial concert. By this time the public's brief enthusiasm for everything Holstian was waning,<sup id="cite_ref-h198164_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198164-118">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> and the piece was not well received in New York. <a href="/wiki/Olin_Downes" title="Olin Downes">Olin Downes</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> opined that "the new score seemed long and undistinguished".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The day after the American performance, Holst conducted the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Birmingham_Symphony_Orchestra" title="City of Birmingham Symphony Orchestra">City of Birmingham Orchestra</a> in the British premiere. <i>The Times</i> acknowledged the bleakness of the work but allowed that it matched Hardy's grim view of the world: "<i>Egdon Heath</i> is not likely to be popular, but it says what the composer wants to say, whether we like it or not, and truth is one aspect of duty."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Holst had been distressed by hostile reviews of some of his earlier works, but he was indifferent to critical opinion of <i>Egdon Heath</i>, which he regarded as, in Adams's phrase, his "most perfectly realized composition".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Towards the end of his life Holst wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/A_Choral_Fantasia_(Holst)" title="A Choral Fantasia (Holst)">Choral Fantasia</a></i> (1930) and he was commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> to write a piece for military band; the resulting prelude and scherzo <i>Hammersmith</i> was a tribute to the place where he had spent most of his life. The composer and critic <a href="/wiki/Colin_Matthews" title="Colin Matthews">Colin Matthews</a> considers the work "as uncompromising in its way as <i>Egdon Heath</i>, discovering, in the words of Imogen Holst, 'in the middle of an over-crowded London&#160;... the same tranquillity that he had found in the solitude of Egdon Heath'".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The work was unlucky in being premiered at a concert that also featured the London premiere of <a href="/wiki/William_Walton" title="William Walton">Walton</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Walton)" title="Belshazzar&#39;s Feast (Walton)">Belshazzar's Feast</a></i>, by which it was somewhat overshadowed.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst wrote a score for a British film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bells_(1931_film)" title="The Bells (1931 film)">The Bells</a></i> (1931), and was amused to be recruited as an extra in a crowd scene.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Both film and score are now lost.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> He wrote a "jazz band piece" that Imogen later arranged for orchestra as <i>Capriccio</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Having composed operas throughout his life with varying success, Holst found for his last opera, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wandering_Scholar" title="The Wandering Scholar">The Wandering Scholar</a></i>, what Matthews calls "the right medium for his oblique sense of humour, writing with economy and directness".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> offered Holst a lectureship for the first six months of 1932. Arriving via New York he was pleased to be reunited with his brother, Emil, whose acting career under the name of Ernest Cossart had taken him to <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a>; but Holst was dismayed by the continual attentions of press interviewers and photographers. He enjoyed his time at Harvard, but was taken ill while there: a <a href="/wiki/Duodenal_ulcer" class="mw-redirect" title="Duodenal ulcer">duodenal ulcer</a> prostrated him for some weeks. He returned to England, joined briefly by his brother for a holiday together in the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> His health declined, and he withdrew further from musical activities. One of his last efforts was to guide the young players of the St Paul's Girls' School orchestra through one of his final compositions, the <i><a href="/wiki/Brook_Green_Suite" title="Brook Green Suite">Brook Green Suite</a></i>, in March 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst died in London on 25 May 1934, at the age of 59, of <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> following an operation on his ulcer.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> His ashes were interred at <a href="/wiki/Chichester_Cathedral" title="Chichester Cathedral">Chichester Cathedral</a> in Sussex, close to the memorial to Thomas Weelkes, his favourite Tudor composer.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Bishop <a href="/wiki/George_Bell_(bishop)" title="George Bell (bishop)">George Bell</a> gave the memorial oration at the funeral, and Vaughan Williams conducted music by Holst and himself.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Gustav_Holst" title="List of compositions by Gustav Holst">List of compositions by Gustav Holst</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style">Style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Holst's absorption of folksong, not only in the melodic sense but in terms of its simplicity and economy of expression,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> helped to develop a style that many of his contemporaries, even admirers, found austere and cerebral.<sup id="cite_ref-H664_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H664-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OCM_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-133">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> This is contrary to the popular identification of Holst with <i>The Planets</i>, which Matthews believes has masked his status as a composer of genuine originality.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Against charges of coldness in the music, Imogen cites Holst's characteristic "sweeping modal tunes mov[ing] reassuringly above the steps of a descending bass",<sup id="cite_ref-H664_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H664-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Michael_Kennedy_(music_critic)" title="Michael Kennedy (music critic)">Michael Kennedy</a> points to the 12 <a href="/wiki/Humbert_Wolfe" title="Humbert Wolfe">Humbert Wolfe</a> settings of 1929, and the 12 Welsh folksong settings for unaccompanied chorus of 1930–31, as works of true warmth.<sup id="cite_ref-OCM_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-133">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the characteristics that Holst employed — unconventional <a href="/wiki/Time_signatures" class="mw-redirect" title="Time signatures">time signatures</a>, rising and falling scales, <a href="/wiki/Ostinato" title="Ostinato">ostinato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bitonality" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitonality">bitonality</a> and occasional <a href="/wiki/Polytonality" title="Polytonality">polytonality</a> — set him apart from other English composers.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Vaughan Williams remarked that Holst always said in his music what he wished to say, directly and concisely; "He was not afraid of being obvious when the occasion demanded, nor did he hesitate to be remote when remoteness expressed his purpose".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy has surmised that Holst's economy of style was in part a product of the composer's poor health: "the effort of writing it down compelled an artistic economy which some felt was carried too far".<sup id="cite_ref-OCM_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-133">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> However, as an experienced instrumentalist and orchestra member, Holst understood music from the standpoint of his players and made sure that, however challenging, their parts were always practicable.<sup id="cite_ref-Short336_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Short336-135">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> According to his pupil <a href="/wiki/Jane_Joseph" title="Jane Joseph">Jane Joseph</a>, Holst fostered in performance "a spirit of practical comradeship&#160;... none could know better than he the boredom possible to a professional player, and the music that rendered boredom impossible".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_works">Early works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Early works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
