<p>Although Holst wrote a large number of works—particularly songs—during his student days and early adulthood, almost everything he wrote before 1904 he later classified as derivative "early horrors".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-H661_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H661-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the composer and critic Colin Matthews recognises even in these apprentice works an "instinctive orchestral flair".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Of the few pieces from this period which demonstrate some originality, Matthews pinpoints the G minor String Trio of 1894 (unperformed until 1974) as the first underivative work produced by Holst.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews84_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews84-138">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Matthews and Imogen Holst each highlight the "Elegy" movement in <i>The Cotswold Symphony</i> (1899–1900) as among the more accomplished of the apprentice works, and Imogen discerns glimpses of her father's real self in the 1899 <i>Suite de ballet</i> and the <i>Ave Maria</i> of 1900. She and Matthews have asserted that Holst found his genuine voice in his setting of Whitman's verses, <i>The Mystic Trumpeter</i> (1904), in which the trumpet calls that characterise Mars in <i>The Planets</i> are briefly anticipated.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-H661_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H661-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In this work, Holst first employs the technique of bitonality—the use of two keys simultaneously.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_years">Experimental years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Experimental years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the beginning of the 20th century, according to Matthews, it appeared that Holst might follow <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">Schoenberg</a> into late <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romanticism</a>. Instead, as Holst recognised afterwards, his encounter with Purcell's <i>Dido and Aeneas</i> prompted his searching for a "musical idiom of the English language";<sup id="cite_ref-GHolstWhit23_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GHolstWhit23-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> the folksong revival became a further catalyst for Holst to seek inspiration from other sources during the first decade or so of the new century.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_period">Indian period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Indian period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holst's interest in Indian mythology, shared by many of his contemporaries, first became musically evident in the opera <i>Sita</i> (1901–06).<sup id="cite_ref-H1_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1-139">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> During the opera's long gestation, Holst worked on other Indian-themed pieces. These included <i>Maya</i> (1901) for violin and piano, regarded by the composer and writer Raymond Head as "an insipid salon-piece whose musical language is dangerously close to Stephen Adams".<sup id="cite_ref-H1_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1-139">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;n 13&#93;</a></sup> Then, through Vaughan Williams, Holst discovered and became an admirer of the music of <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Ravel" title="Maurice Ravel">Ravel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-HolstEB_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HolstEB-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> whom he considered a "model of purity" on the level with Haydn,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> another composer he greatly admired.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The combined influence of Ravel, <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> spiritualism and English folk tunes<sup id="cite_ref-HolstEB_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HolstEB-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> enabled Holst to get beyond the once all-consuming influences of Wagner and Richard Strauss and to forge his own style. Imogen Holst has acknowledged Holst's own suggestion (written to Vaughan Williams): "[O]ne ought to follow Wagner until he leads you to fresh things". She notes that although much of his grand opera, <i>Sita</i>, is "'good old Wagnerian bawling'&#160;... towards the end a change comes over the music, and the beautifully calm phrases of the hidden chorus representing the Voice of the Earth are in Holst's own language."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Rubbra, the publication in 1911 of Holst's Rig Veda Hymns was a landmark event in the composer's development: "Before this, Holst's music had, indeed, shown the clarity of utterance which has always been his characteristic, but harmonically there was little to single him out as an important figure in modern music."<sup id="cite_ref-r30_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-r30-67">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Dickinson describes these <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">vedic</a> settings as pictorial rather than religious; although the quality is variable the sacred texts clearly "touched vital springs in the composer's imagination".<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> While the music of Holst's Indian verse settings remained generally western in character, in some of the vedic settings he experimented with Indian <i>raga</i> (scales).<sup id="cite_ref-H2_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H2-147">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The chamber opera <i><a href="/wiki/Savitri_(opera)" title="Savitri (opera)">Savitri</a></i> (1908) is written for three solo voices, a small hidden female chorus, and an instrumental combination of two flutes, a cor anglais and a double string quartet.<sup id="cite_ref-H3_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H3-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The music critic <a href="/wiki/John_Warrack" title="John Warrack">John Warrack</a> comments on the "extraordinary expressive subtlety" with which Holst deploys the sparse forces: "...&#160;[T]he two unaccompanied vocal lines opening the work skilfully convey the relationship between Death, steadily advancing through the forest, and Savitri, her frightened answers fluttering round him, unable to escape his harmonic pull."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Head describes the work as unique in its time for its compact intimacy, and considers it Holst's most successful attempt to end the domination of Wagnerian <a href="/wiki/Chromaticism" title="Chromaticism">chromaticism</a> in his music.<sup id="cite_ref-H3_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H3-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Dickinson considers it a significant step, "not towards opera, but towards an idiomatic pursuit of [Holst's] vision".<sup id="cite_ref-D20_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D20-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Of the Kālidāsa texts, Dickinson dismisses <i>The Cloud Messenger</i> (1910–12) as an "accumulation of desultory incidents, opportunistic dramatic episodes and ecstatic outpourings" which illustrate the composer's creative confusion during that period; the <i>Two Eastern Pictures</i> (1911), in Dickinson's view, provide "a more memorable final impression of Kālidāsa".<sup id="cite_ref-D20_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D20-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Folksong_and_other_influences">Folksong and other influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Folksong and other influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holst's settings of Indian texts formed only a part of his compositional output in the period 1900 to 1914. A highly significant factor in his musical development was the English folksong revival, evident in the orchestral suite <i>A Somerset Rhapsody</i> (1906–07), a work that was originally to be based around eleven folksong themes; this was later reduced to four.<sup id="cite_ref-D192_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D192-150">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Observing the work's kinship with Vaughan Williams's <i>Norfolk Rhapsody</i>, Dickinson remarks that, with its firm overall structure, Holst's composition "rises beyond the level of&#160;... a song-selection".<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Imogen acknowledges that Holst's discovery of English folksongs "transformed his orchestral writing", and that the composition of <i>A Somerset Rhapsody</i> did much to banish the chromaticisms that had dominated his early compositions.<sup id="cite_ref-H661_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H661-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In the <i>Two Songs without Words</i> of 1906, Holst showed that he could create his own original music using the folk idiom.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> An orchestral folksong fantasy <i>Songs of the West</i>, also written in 1906, was withdrawn by the composer and never published, although it emerged in the 1980s in the form of an arrangement for wind band by <a href="/wiki/James_Curnow" title="James Curnow">James Curnow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>

<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst_First_Suite_March.ogg" title="File:Holst First Suite March.ogg">March from Holst's First Suite in E-flat for Military Band</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="172.57185941043" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Holst_First_Suite_March.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Holst_First_Suite_March.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (157 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="157185" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/29/Holst_First_Suite_March.ogg/Holst_First_Suite_March.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="206216" /></audio></div></div>

<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the years before the First World War, Holst composed in a variety of genres. Matthews considers the evocation of a North African town in the <i><a href="/wiki/Beni_Mora" title="Beni Mora">Beni Mora</a></i> suite of 1908 the composer's most individual work to that date; the third movement gives a preview of <a href="/wiki/Minimal_music" title="Minimal music">minimalism</a> in its constant repetition of a four-bar theme. Holst wrote two suites for military band, in <a href="/wiki/First_Suite_in_E-flat_for_Military_Band" title="First Suite in E-flat for Military Band">E flat (1909)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Suite_in_F_for_Military_Band" title="Second Suite in F for Military Band">F major (1911)</a> respectively, the first of which became and remains a brass-band staple.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This piece, a highly original and substantial musical work, was a signal departure from what Short describes as "the usual transcriptions and operatic selections which pervaded the band repertoire".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Also in 1911 he wrote <i>Hecuba's Lament</i>, a setting of <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Murray" title="Gilbert Murray">Gilbert Murray</a>'s translation from <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a> built on a seven-beat refrain designed, says Dickinson, to represent <a href="/wiki/Hecuba" title="Hecuba">Hecuba</a>'s defiance of divine wrath.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In 1912 Holst composed two psalm settings, in which he experimented with <a href="/wiki/Plainsong" title="Plainsong">plainsong</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-H662_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H662-156">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> the same year saw the enduringly popular <i>St Paul's Suite</i> (a "gay but retrogressive" piece according to Dickinson),<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> and the failure of his large scale orchestral work <i>Phantastes</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Full_flowering">Full flowering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Full flowering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Planets"><i>The Planets</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: The Planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">The Planets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg/220px-Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg/330px-Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg/440px-Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg 2x" data-file-width="556" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-Planets-Saturn-score.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The opening of "Saturn", the fifth movement of <i>The Planets</i></div></div></div>
<p>Holst conceived the idea of <i>The Planets</i> in 1913, partly as a result of his interest in astrology,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;n 14&#93;</a></sup> and also from his determination, despite the failure of <i>Phantastes</i>, to produce a large-scale orchestral work.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The chosen format may have been influenced by Schoenberg's <a href="/wiki/Five_Pieces_for_Orchestra" title="Five Pieces for Orchestra"><i>Fünf Orchesterstücke</i></a>, and shares something of the aesthetic, Matthews suggests, of <a href="/wiki/Claude_Debussy" title="Claude Debussy">Debussy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nocturnes_(Debussy)" title="Nocturnes (Debussy)">Nocturnes</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/La_mer_(Debussy)" title="La mer (Debussy)">La mer</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Holst began composing <i>The Planets</i> in 1914; the movements appeared not quite in their final sequence; "Mars" was the first to be written, followed by "Venus" and "Jupiter". "Saturn", "Uranus" and "Neptune" were all composed during 1915, and "Mercury" was completed in 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each planet is represented with a distinct character; Dickinson observes that "no planet borrows colour from another".<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In "Mars", a persistent, uneven <a href="/wiki/Cell_(music)" title="Cell (music)">rhythmic cell</a> consisting of five beats, combined with trumpet calls and harmonic dissonance provides battle music which Short asserts is unique in its expression of violence and sheer terror, "...&#160;Holst's intention being to portray the reality of warfare rather than to glorify deeds of heroism".<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In "Venus", Holst incorporated music from an abandoned vocal work, <i>A Vigil of Pentecost</i>, to provide the opening; the prevalent mood within the movement is of peaceful resignation and nostalgia.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews84_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews84-138">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> "Mercury" is dominated by uneven metres and rapid changes of theme, to represent the speedy flight of the winged messenger.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> "Jupiter" is renowned for its central melody, "<a href="/wiki/Thaxted_(tune)" title="Thaxted (tune)">Thaxted</a>", in Dickinson's view "a fantastic relaxation in which many retain a far from sneaking delight".<sup id="cite_ref-Dickinson123_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson123-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Dickinson and other critics have decried the later use of the tune in the patriotic hymn "<a href="/wiki/I_Vow_to_Thee,_My_Country" title="I Vow to Thee, My Country">I Vow to Thee, My Country</a>"—despite Holst's full complicity.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dickinson123_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson123-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;n 15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For "Saturn", Holst again used a previously-composed vocal piece, <i>Dirge and Hymeneal</i>, as the basis for the movement, where repeated chords represent the relentless approach of old age.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> "Uranus", which follows, has elements of <a href="/wiki/Hector_Berlioz" title="Hector Berlioz">Berlioz</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Symphonie_fantastique" title="Symphonie fantastique">Symphonie fantastique</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dukas" title="Paul Dukas">Dukas</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sorcerer%27s_Apprentice" title="The Sorcerer&#39;s Apprentice">The Sorcerer's Apprentice</a></i>, in its depiction of the magician who "disappears in a whiff of smoke as the sonic impetus of the movement diminishes from <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(music)#Relative_loudness" title="Dynamics (music)">fff</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(music)#Relative_loudness" title="Dynamics (music)">ppp</a> in the space of a few bars".<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> "Neptune", the final movement, concludes with a wordless female chorus gradually receding, an effect which Warrack likens to "unresolved timelessness&#160;... never ending, since space does not end, but drifting away into eternal silence".<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Apart from his concession with "I Vow to Thee..."', Holst insisted on the unity of the whole work, and opposed the performance of individual movements.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Imogen wrote that the piece had "suffered from being quoted in snippets as background music".<sup id="cite_ref-H663_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H663-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maturity">Maturity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Maturity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="pencil drawing of Holst in middle age" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg/130px-Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg" width="130" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg/195px-Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg/260px-Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="379" data-file-height="583" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holst-by-rothenstein-1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Holst drawn by <a href="/wiki/William_Rothenstein" title="William Rothenstein">William Rothenstein</a>, 1920</div></div></div>
<p>During and after the composition of <i>The Planets</i>, Holst wrote or arranged numerous vocal and choral works, many of them for the wartime Thaxted Whitsun Festivals, 1916–18. They include the <i>Six Choral Folksongs</i> of 1916, based on <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a> tunes, of which "Swansea Town", with its "sophisticated tone", is deemed by Dickinson to be the most memorable.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Holst downplayed such music as "a limited form of art" in which "mannerisms are almost inevitable";<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> the composer Alan Gibbs, however, believes Holst's set at least equal to Vaughan Williams's <i>Five English Folk Songs</i> of 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst's first major work after <i>The Planets</i> was the <i>Hymn of Jesus</i>, completed in 1917. The words are from a <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostic</a> text, the apocryphal Acts of St John, using a translation from the Greek which Holst prepared with assistance from Clifford Bax and Jane Joseph.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Head comments on the innovative character of the <i>Hymn</i>: "At a stroke Holst had cast aside the Victorian and Edwardian sentimental oratorio, and created the precursor of the kind of works that <a href="/wiki/John_Tavener" title="John Tavener">John Tavener</a>, for example, was to write in the 1970s".<sup id="cite_ref-Hymn_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hymn-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Matthews has written that the <i>Hymn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s "ecstatic" quality is matched in English music "perhaps only by Tippett's <i>The Vision of Saint Augustine</i>";<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> the musical elements include plainsong, two choirs distanced from each other to emphasise dialogue, dance episodes and "explosive chordal dislocations".<sup id="cite_ref-Hymn_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hymn-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Ode to Death</i> (1918–19), the quiet, resigned mood is seen by Matthews as an "abrupt volte-face" after the life-enhancing spirituality of the <i>Hymn</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Warrack refers to its aloof tranquillity;<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Imogen Holst believed the <i>Ode</i> expressed Holst's private attitude to death.<sup id="cite_ref-H663_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H663-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The piece has rarely been performed since its premiere in 1922, although the composer <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Walker_(composer)" title="Ernest Walker (composer)">Ernest Walker</a> thought it was Holst's finest work to that date.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The influential critic <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Newman" title="Ernest Newman">Ernest Newman</a> considered <i>The Perfect Fool</i> "the best of modern British operas",<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> but its unusually short length (about an hour) and parodic, whimsical nature—described by <i>The Times</i> as "a brilliant puzzle"—put it outside the operatic mainstream.<sup id="cite_ref-timespf_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timespf-115">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Only the ballet music from the opera, which <i>The Times</i> called "the most brilliant thing in a work glittering with brilliant moments", has been regularly performed since 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Holst's libretto attracted much criticism, although <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Evans_(music_critic)" title="Edwin Evans (music critic)">Edwin Evans</a> remarked on the rare treat in opera of being able to hear the words being sung.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_works">Later works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Later works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg/240px-Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg" width="240" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg/360px-Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg/480px-Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="673" data-file-height="595" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Moritz_Retzsch_Henry_IV_part_1_act_2_sc_4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Boar's Head" scene from <i>Henry IV Part I (1853 outline)</i></div></div></div>
<p>Before his enforced rest in 1924, Holst demonstrated a new interest in <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a>, in his <i>Fugal Overture</i> of 1922 for full orchestra and the <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism_(music)" title="Neoclassicism (music)">neo-classical</a><i> Fugal Concerto</i> of 1923, for flute, oboe and strings.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In his final decade he mixed song settings and minor pieces with major works and occasional new departures; the 1925 <i>Terzetto</i> for flute, violin and oboe, each instrument playing in a different key, is cited by Imogen as Holst's only successful <a href="/wiki/Chamber_music" title="Chamber music">chamber work</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Of the <i>Choral Symphony</i> completed in 1924, Matthews writes that, after several movements of real quality, the finale is a rambling anticlimax.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Holst's penultimate opera, <i>At the Boar's Head</i> (1924), is based on tavern scenes from Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_1" title="Henry IV, Part 1">Henry IV, Parts 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_2" title="Henry IV, Part 2">2</a></i>. The music, which is largely derived from old English melodies gleaned from Cecil Sharp and other collections, has pace and verve;<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> the contemporary critic Harvey Grace discounted the lack of originality, a facet which he said "can be shown no less convincingly by a composer's handling of material than by its invention".<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Egdon Heath</i> (1927) was Holst's first major orchestral work after <i>The Planets</i>. Matthews summarises the music as "elusive and unpredictable [with] three main elements: a pulseless wandering melody [for strings], a sad brass processional, and restless music for strings and oboe." The mysterious dance towards the end is, says Matthews, "the strangest moment in a strange work".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Richard Greene in <i><a href="/wiki/Music_%26_Letters" title="Music &amp; Letters">Music &amp; Letters</a></i> describes the piece as "a <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">larghetto</a> dance in a <a href="/wiki/Siciliana" title="Siciliana">siciliano</a> rhythm with a simple, stepwise, rocking melody", but lacking the power of <i>The Planets</i> and, at times, monotonous to the listener.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> A more popular success was the <i>Moorside Suite</i> for brass band, written as a test piece for the National Brass Band Festival championships of 1928. While written within the traditions of north-country brass-band music, the suite, Short says, bears Holst's unmistakable imprint, "from the skipping <a href="/wiki/Time_signature#Most_frequent_time_signatures" title="Time signature">6/8</a> of the opening Scherzo, to the vigorous melodic fourths of the concluding March, the intervening Nocturne bearing a family resemblance to the slow-moving procession of <i>Saturn</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> 'A Moorside Suite' has undergone major revisionism in the article 'Symphony Within: rehearing Holst's 'A Moorside Suite' by Stephen Arthur Allen in the Winter 2017 edition of 'The Musical Times'.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> As with 'Egdon Heath' – commissioned as a symphony – the article reveals the symphonic nature of this brass-band work. 
</p><p>After this, Holst tackled his final attempt at opera in a cheerful vein, with <i>The Wandering Scholar</i> (1929–30), to a text by Clifford Bax. Imogen refers to the music as "Holst at his best in a scherzando (playful) frame of mind";<sup id="cite_ref-H664_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H664-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Vaughan Williams commented on the lively, folksy rhythms: "Do you think there's a <i>little</i> bit too much 6/8 in the opera?"<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Short observes that the opening motif makes several reappearances without being identified with a particular character, but imposes musical unity on the work.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holst composed few large-scale works in his final years. <i>A Choral Fantasia</i> of 1930 was written for the <a href="/wiki/Three_Choirs_Festival" title="Three Choirs Festival">Three Choirs Festival</a> at <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>; beginning and ending with a soprano soloist, the work, also involving chorus, strings, brass and percussion, includes a substantial organ solo which, says Imogen Holst, "knows something of the 'colossal and mysterious' loneliness of <i>Egdon Heath</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Apart from his final uncompleted symphony, Holst's remaining works were for small forces; the eight <i>Canons</i> of 1932 were dedicated to his pupils, though in Imogen's view that they present a formidable challenge to the most professional of singers. The <i>Brook Green Suite</i> (1932), written for the orchestra of St Paul's School, was a late companion piece to the <i>St Paul's Suite</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-H663_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H663-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Lyric Movement</i> for viola and small orchestra (1933) was written for <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Tertis" title="Lionel Tertis">Lionel Tertis</a>. Quiet and contemplative, and requiring little virtuosity from the soloist, the piece was slow to gain popularity among violists.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Robin Hull, in <i>Penguin Music Magazine</i>, praised the work's "clear beauty—impossible to mistake for the art of any other composer"; in Dickinson's view, however, it remains "a frail creation".<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Holst's final composition, the orchestral scherzo movement of a projected symphony, contains features characteristic of much of Holst's earlier music—"a summing up of Holst's orchestral art", according to Short.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Dickinson suggests that the somewhat casual collection of material in the work gives little indication of the symphony that might have been written.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recordings">Recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Holst made some recordings, conducting his own music. For the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graphophone_Company" title="Columbia Graphophone Company">Columbia</a> company he recorded <i>Beni Mora</i>, the <i>Marching Song</i> and the complete <i>Planets</i> with the <a href="/wiki/London_Symphony_Orchestra" title="London Symphony Orchestra">London Symphony Orchestra</a> (LSO) in 1922, using the <a href="/wiki/Recording_studio#1890s_to_1930s" title="Recording studio">acoustic process</a>. The limitations of early recording prevented the gradual fade-out of women's voices at the end of "Neptune", and the lower strings had to be replaced by a tuba to obtain an effective bass sound.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> With an anonymous string orchestra Holst recorded the <i>St Paul's Suite</i> and <i>Country Song</i> in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Columbia's main rival, <a href="/wiki/The_Gramophone_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="The Gramophone Company">HMV</a>, issued recordings of some of the same repertoire, with an unnamed orchestra conducted by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Coates_(musician)" title="Albert Coates (musician)">Albert Coates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> When electrical recording came in, with dramatically improved recording quality, Holst and the LSO re-recorded <i>The Planets</i> for Columbia in 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early <a href="/wiki/Long_playing_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Long playing record">LP</a> era little of Holst's music was available on disc. Only six of his works are listed in the 1955 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Record_Guide" title="The Record Guide">The Record Guide</a></i>: <i>The Planets</i> (recordings under Boult on HMV and <a href="/wiki/Nixa_Records" title="Nixa Records">Nixa</a>, and another under <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Sargent" title="Malcolm Sargent">Sir Malcolm Sargent</a> on <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca</a>); the <i>Perfect Fool</i> ballet music; the <i>St Paul's Suite</i>; and three short choral pieces.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> In the stereo LP and CD eras numerous recordings of <i>The Planets</i> were issued, performed by orchestras and conductors from round the world. By the early years of the 21st century most of the major and many of the minor orchestral and choral works had been issued on disc. The 2008 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Penguin_Guide_to_Recorded_Classical_Music" title="The Penguin Guide to Recorded Classical Music">The Penguin Guide to Recorded Classical Music</a></i> contained seven pages of listings of Holst's works on CD.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Of the operas, <i>Savitri</i>, <i>The Wandering Scholar</i>, and <i>At the Boar's Head</i> have been recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:246px;&#32;; background-color: #c6dbf7;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"[Holst's] influence is lasting in the work of all of us who value directness and sincerity and who view music not so much a secret preserve for the leisured few as a vital part of everyday life"</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><i>A tribute from <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Rubbra" title="Edmund Rubbra">Edmund Rubbra</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Warrack emphasises that Holst acquired an instinctive understanding—perhaps more so than any English composer—of the importance of folksong. In it he found "a new concept not only of how melody might be organized, but of what the implications were for the development of a mature artistic language".<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Holst did not found or lead a school of composition; nevertheless, he exercised influences over both contemporaries and successors. According to Short, Vaughan Williams described Holst as "the greatest influence on my music",<sup id="cite_ref-Short336_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Short336-135">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> although Matthews asserts that each influenced the other equally.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_5-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Among later composers, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tippett" title="Michael Tippett">Michael Tippett</a> is acknowledged by Short as Holst's "most significant artistic successor", both in terms of compositional style and because Tippett, who succeeded Holst as director of music at Morley College, maintained the spirit of Holst's music there.<sup id="cite_ref-Short336_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Short336-135">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Of an early encounter with Holst, Tippett later wrote: "Holst seemed to look right inside me, with an acute spiritual vision".<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy observes that "a new generation of listeners&#160;... recognized in Holst the fount of much that they admired in the music of Britten and Tippett".<sup id="cite_ref-OCM_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OCM-133">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Holst's pupil Edmund Rubbra acknowledged how he and other younger English composers had adopted Holst's economy of style: "With what enthusiasm did we pare down our music to the very bone".<sup id="cite_ref-H664_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H664-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Short cites other English composers who are in debt to Holst, in particular William Walton and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Britten" title="Benjamin Britten">Benjamin Britten</a>, and suggests that Holst's influence may have been felt further afield.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;n 16&#93;</a></sup> Above all, Short recognises Holst as a composer for the people, who believed it was a composer's duty to provide music for practical purposes—festivals, celebrations, ceremonies, Christmas carols or simple hymn tunes. Thus, says Short, "many people who may never have heard any of [Holst's] major works&#160;... have nevertheless derived great pleasure from hearing or singing such small masterpieces as the carol '<a href="/wiki/In_the_Bleak_Midwinter" title="In the Bleak Midwinter">In the Bleak Midwinter</a>'".<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 27&#160;September 2009, after a weekend of concerts at Chichester Cathedral in memory of Holst, a new memorial was unveiled to mark the 75th anniversary of the composer's death. It is inscribed with words from the text of <i>The Hymn of Jesus</i>: "The heavenly spheres make music for us".<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In April 2011 a BBC television documentary, <i>Holst: In the Bleak Midwinter</i>, charted Holst's life with particular reference to his support for socialism and the cause of working people.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gustav_Holst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes and references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Notes</b>

<li id="cite_note-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-159">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Holst was reading <a href="/wiki/Alan_Leo" title="Alan Leo">Alan Leo</a>'s booklet <i>What is a Horoscope?</i> at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Alan Gibbs, who edited Dickinson's book, remarks in a footnote that, perhaps fortunately, neither Dickinson nor Imogen was alive to hear the "deplorable 1990s version" of the Jupiter tune, sung as an anthem at the Rugby World Cup.<sup id="cite_ref-Dickinson123_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson123-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Short observes that the rising fourths of "Jupiter" can be heard in <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Copland" title="Aaron Copland">Copland</a>'s <i>Appalachian Spring</i>, and suggests that the <i>Hymn of Jesus</i> might be considered as a forerunner of <a href="/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky" title="Igor Stravinsky">Stravinsky</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony_of_Psalms" title="Symphony of Psalms">Symphony of Psalms</a></i> "and the hieratic serial cantatas", though admitting that "it is doubtful whether Stravinsky was familiar with, or even aware of this work".<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
</ol></div>
